<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Sleaford&amp;params=52.996_N_-0.413_E_region:GB_type:city(17671)"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">52°59′46″N</span> <span class="longitude">0°24′47″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">52.996°N 0.413°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">52.996; -0.413</span></span></span></a></span><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Sleaford&amp;params=52.996_N_-0.413_E_region:GB_type:city(17671)"><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">52°59′46″N</span> <span class="longitude">0°24′47″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">52.996°N 0.413°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">52.996; -0.413</span></span></span></a></span></span></span></td>

</table>
<p><b>Sleaford</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Market_town" title="Market town">market town</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civil_parishes_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil parishes in England">civil parish</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a>, England. It is on the edge of the fertile <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">Fenlands</a>, about 11 miles (18&#160;km) north-east of <a href="/wiki/Grantham" title="Grantham">Grantham</a>, 16 miles (26&#160;km) west of <a href="/wiki/Boston,_Lincolnshire" title="Boston, Lincolnshire">Boston</a>, and 17 miles (27&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Lincoln,_England" title="Lincoln, England">Lincoln</a>. With a population of 17,671 at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 Census</a>, the town is the largest settlement in the <a href="/wiki/North_Kesteven" title="North Kesteven">North Kesteven</a> district. Bypassed by the <a href="/wiki/A17_road_(England)" title="A17 road (England)">A17</a> and the <a href="/wiki/A15_road_(England)" title="A15 road (England)">A15</a>, it is connected to Lincoln, <a href="/wiki/Newark-on-Trent" title="Newark-on-Trent">Newark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King's Lynn">King's Lynn</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_railway_station" title="Sleaford railway station">Sleaford railway station</a> is on the <a href="/wiki/Grantham_to_Skegness_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Grantham to Skegness Line">Nottingham to Skegness (via Grantham)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peterborough_to_Lincoln_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Peterborough to Lincoln Line">Peterborough to Lincoln Lines</a>.</p>
<p>The first settlement formed in the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> where a prehistoric track crossed the <a href="/wiki/River_Slea" title="River Slea">River Slea</a>. It was a tribal centre and home to a mint for the <a href="/wiki/Corieltauvi" title="Corieltauvi">Corieltauvi</a> in the 1st centuries BC and AD. Evidence of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon</a> settlement has been uncovered, and by the late Saxon period the town was an economic and jurisdictional centre with a court and market. In the medieval period, records differentiate between Old and New Sleaford, the latter emerging in the areas around the present day market place and <a href="/wiki/St_Denys%27_Church,_Sleaford" title="St Denys' Church, Sleaford">St Denys' Church</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Castle" title="Sleaford Castle">Sleaford Castle</a> was constructed in the 12th century for the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lincoln" title="Bishop of Lincoln">Bishops of Lincoln</a>, who owned the manor. Granted the right to hold a market in the mid-12th century, New Sleaford developed into a market town and became locally important in the wool trade, while Old Sleaford declined.</p>
<p>From the 16th century, the landowners were the Carre family, who operated tight control over the town, and it grew little in the early modern period. The manor passed from the Carre family to the <a href="/wiki/Marquess_of_Bristol" title="Marquess of Bristol">Hervey family</a> by the marriage of Isabella Carre to <a href="/wiki/John_Hervey,_1st_Earl_of_Bristol" title="John Hervey, 1st Earl of Bristol">John Hervey, 1st Earl of Bristol</a>, in 1688. The town's <a href="/wiki/Common_land" title="Common land">common land</a> and fields were <a href="/wiki/Enclosure_(legal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclosure (legal)">legally enclosed</a> by 1794, giving ownership mostly to the Hervey family; this coincided with making the Slea into a canal, and heralded the first steps towards modern industry. The <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Navigation" title="Sleaford Navigation">Sleaford Navigation</a> brought economic growth until it was superseded by the railways in the mid-1850s. In the 20th century, the sale of farmland around Sleaford by Bristol Estates led to the development of large housing estates. The subsequent availability of affordable housing combined with the town's educational facilities and low crime rates made it an attractive destination for home-buyers. As a result, the town's population underwent the fastest growth of any town in the county in the 1990s.</p>
<p>Sleaford was primarily an agricultural town until the 20th century, supporting a cattle market, with seed companies, such as Hubbard and Phillips, and Sharpes International Seeds, being established in the late 19th century. The arrival of the railway made the town favourable for malting. Industry has declined, and in 2011 the most common occupations are in wholesale and retail trade, health and social care, public administration and defence and manufacturing. Regeneration of the town centre has led to the redevelopment of the old industrial areas, including the construction of <a href="/wiki/The_National_Centre_for_Craft_%26_Design" title="The National Centre for Craft &amp; Design">the National Centre for Craft &amp; Design</a> on an old wharf.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The earliest records of the place-name Sleaford are found in a charter of 852 as <i>Slioford</i> and in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a> as <i>Sliowaford</i>. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> (1086), it is recorded as <i>Eslaforde</i> and in the early 13th century as <i>Sliforde</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-2">[2]</a></sup> In the 13th century <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Fees" title="Book of Fees">Book of Fees</a></i> the name appears as <i>Lafford</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The name is formed from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> words <i>sliow</i> and <i>ford</i>, which together mean 'ford over a muddy or slimy river'.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early">Early</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corieltauvistater.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Corieltauvistater.jpg/220px-Corieltauvistater.jpg" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Corieltauvistater.jpg/330px-Corieltauvistater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Corieltauvistater.jpg/440px-Corieltauvistater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2606" data-file-height="1342" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corieltauvistater.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An electrum <a href="/wiki/Stater" title="Stater">stater</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Corieltauvi" title="Corieltauvi">Corieltauvi</a>, probably struck at Sleaford in the mid-1st century BC. Diameter 17–19&#160;mm.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Archaeological material from the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> and earlier has been recovered and excavations have shown that there was unsustained late-<a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> and Bronze Age human activity in the vicinity.<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-6_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-6-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The earliest known permanent settlement dates from the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and began where a track running northwards from <a href="/wiki/Bourne,_Lincolnshire" title="Bourne, Lincolnshire">Bourne</a> crossed the River Slea.<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-6_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-6-4">[4]</a></sup> Although only sparse pottery evidence has been found for the middle Iron Age period, 4,290 pellet mould fragments, likely used for minting and dated to 50&#160;BC–AD 50, have been uncovered south east of the modern town centre, south of a crossing of the River Slea and near Mareham Lane in Old Sleaford. The largest of its kind in Europe, the deposit has led archaeologists to consider that the site in Old Sleaford was probably one of the largest <a href="/wiki/Corieltauvi" title="Corieltauvi">Corieltauvian</a> settlements during this period and may have been a tribal centre.<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-6_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-6-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman occupation of Britain</a> (AD 43–409), the settlement was "extensive and of considerable importance."<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Its location along the fen-edge may have made it economically and administratively significant as a centre for managers and owners of fenland estates.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> There is evidence to suggest that a road connected Old Sleaford to <a href="/wiki/Heckington" title="Heckington">Heckington</a> (about 4.5 miles (7.2&#160;km) east), where Roman tile kilns have been uncovered and may imply the presence of a market at Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-10_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-10-9">[9]</a></sup> When the first roads were constructed by the Romans, Sleaford was bypassed due to it being "less conveniently located" and more "geared to native needs".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> A smaller road, <a href="/wiki/Mareham_Lane" title="Mareham Lane">Mareham Lane</a>, which the Romans renewed, ran through Old Sleaford, and southwards along the fen edge, towards Bourne. Where it passed through Old Sleaford, excavations have revealed a large stone-built domestic residence, associated farm buildings, corn-driers, ovens and field systems, all from the Roman period, and a number of burials.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Other Roman remains, including a burial, have been excavated in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:214px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png/212px-Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png" width="212" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png/318px-Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png/425px-Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png 2x" data-file-width="477" data-file-height="647" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Sleaford_Castle.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plan of Sleaford Castle, made in 1872.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is little evidence of continuous settlement between the late Roman and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon England">Anglo-Saxon</a> periods<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-10_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-10-9">[9]</a></sup> but the Saxons did establish themselves eventually. South of the modern town, a 6th–7th century cemetery has been uncovered containing an estimated 600 burials, many showing signs of <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">pagan</a> burial rites.<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-10_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-10-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[n 1]</a></sup> The now ruined Church of St Giles/All Saints at Old Sleaford has been discovered<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-10_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-10-9">[9]</a></sup> and excavations of the market place in 1979 uncovered Anglo-Saxon remains from the 8th–9th centuries, indicating some form of enclosure with domestic features.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> The earliest documentary reference to Sleaford is in a charter from the 9th century,<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-11_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-11-17">[16]</a></sup> when it was owned by Medehamstede Abbey in <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercian</a> royal foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> There is little evidence of estate structure until the late Saxon period,<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-10_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-10-9">[9]</a></sup> but there may have been a market and court in pre-<a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Conquest</a> Sleaford and it was probably an economic and jurisdictional centre for surrounding settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> The Slea played an important role in the town's economy: it never ran dry nor froze, and by the 11th century a dozen <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermills</a> lined its banks. The mills and others in nearby <a href="/wiki/Quarrington,_Lincolnshire" title="Quarrington, Lincolnshire">Quarrington</a> and the lost hamlet of Millsthorpe, constituted the "most important mill cluster in Lincolnshire".<sup id="cite_ref-:2_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-20">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later in the Middle Ages, the Romano-British settlement became known as "Old Sleaford", while "New Sleaford" was used to describe the settlement centred on <a href="/wiki/St_Denys%27_Church,_Sleaford" title="St Denys' Church, Sleaford">St Denys' Church</a> and the market place.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> (1086) has two entries under <i>Eslaforde</i> (Sleaford) recording land held by <a href="/wiki/Ramsey_Abbey" title="Ramsey Abbey">Ramsey Abbey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lincoln" title="Bishop of Lincoln">Bishop of Lincoln</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[n 2]</a></sup> The location of the manors recorded in <i>Domesday</i> is not made clear in the text. One theory endorsed by <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Beresford" title="Maurice Beresford">Maurice Beresford</a> is that they focused on the settlement at Old Sleaford because of evidence that New Sleaford was planted in the 12th century by the bishop to increase his income,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[n 3]</a></sup> which was associated with the construction of <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Castle" title="Sleaford Castle">Sleaford Castle</a> between 1123 and 1139.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> Beresford's theory has been criticised by the local historians Christine Mahany and David Roffe<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[n 4]</a></sup> who have reinterpreted the <i>Domesday</i> material and argued that, by 1086, the Bishop's manor included a church and associated settlement in what became New Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The right to hold a fair on the feast day of St Denis was granted by a charter of King <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen</a> to <a href="/wiki/Alexander,_Bishop_of_Lincoln" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander, Bishop of Lincoln">Alexander, Bishop of Lincoln</a>, in 1136–40. Between 1154 and 1165, <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry II</a> granted the bishop of Lincoln the right to hold a market at Sleaford; bishop <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Sutton_(bishop)" title="Oliver Sutton (bishop)">Oliver Sutton</a> argued in 1281 that his right to hold a market and fair had existed since time immemorial. In 1329, <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a> confirmed the market and, in 1401, <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV</a> granted the bishop fairs on the feast days of St Denis and St Peter's Chains.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup> A survey of 1258 is the first to mention <a href="/wiki/Burgage" title="Burgage">burgage</a> tenure;<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> tenants in the nearby hamlet of Holdingham held tofts with other land, while those in New Sleaford held only tofts, indicating that <a href="/wiki/Demesne" title="Demesne">demesne</a> farming centred on the hamlet.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-32">[28]</a></sup> The town later had at least two <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a> comparable to those found in developed towns.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-33">[29]</a></sup> However, there was no formal charter outlining the town's freedoms;<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._24_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._24-34">[30]</a></sup> it was not a centre of trade and tight control by the bishops meant the economy was primarily geared to serve them. Hence, it retained a strong tradition of demesne farming well into the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:17_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-35">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup> As the economic initiative fell more to the burgesses and middlemen who formed connections with nearby towns, such as <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a>, evidence suggests that Sleaford developed a locally important role in the wool trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Roffe_p._19_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roffe_p._19-37">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> In the Lay Subsidy of 1334, New Sleaford was the wealthiest settlement in the Flaxwell <a href="/wiki/Hundred_(county_division)" title="Hundred (county division)">wapentake</a>, with a value of £16 0s. 8d.<sup>1</sup>/<sub>4</sub>d.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">[35]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Old Sleaford, an "insignificant" place since the end of the Roman period, declined and may have been deserted by the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-lincstothepast.com_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lincstothepast.com-40">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern">Early modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg/220px-Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg/330px-Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg/440px-Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Edward_Carre_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960417.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The tomb of Sir Edward Carre (died 1618) in St Denys' Church</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the late 15th and early 16th centuries, the Hussey family owned the manor of Old Sleaford. <a href="/wiki/John_Hussey,_1st_Baron_Hussey_of_Sleaford" title="John Hussey, 1st Baron Hussey of Sleaford">John Hussey, 1st Baron Hussey of Sleaford</a> was executed for treason for his part in the <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_of_Grace" title="Pilgrimage of Grace">Lincolnshire Rising</a>. The manor and his residence at Old Place reverted to the Crown and were later sold to Robert Carre.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup> George Carre or Carr from Northumberland had settled in Sleaford by 1522 when he was described as a wool merchant.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> His son Robert bought Hussey's land and the castle and manor of New Sleaford from <a href="/wiki/Edward_Clinton,_1st_Earl_of_Lincoln" title="Edward Clinton, 1st Earl of Lincoln">Edward Clinton, 1st Earl of Lincoln</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[n 5]</a></sup> His eldest surviving son Robert, founded <a href="/wiki/Carre%27s_Grammar_School" title="Carre's Grammar School">Carre's Grammar School</a> in 1604, and his youngest son Edward was created a <a href="/wiki/Carr_baronets" title="Carr baronets">baronet</a>; his son founded Sleaford Hospital in 1636.<sup id="cite_ref-:18_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-49">[44]</a></sup> The last male descendent died in 1683 and the heiress, Isabella Carre, married <a href="/wiki/John_Hervey,_1st_Earl_of_Bristol" title="John Hervey, 1st Earl of Bristol">John Hervey, Earl of Bristol</a>, in whose family the estates remained until the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Trollope_Sleaford_p._134_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trollope_Sleaford_p._134-50">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pawley-50_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley-50-51">[46]</a></sup> The Carres and Herveys had a strong influence: in addition to extracting dues from their tenants, they took leading tradesmen to the <a href="/wiki/Exchequer_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchequer Court">Exchequer Court</a> to gain legal force behind their monopoly on charging tolls on market and cattle traders and for driving animals through the town.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Industry was slow to take hold, and by the second half of the 18th century, <a href="/wiki/Cogglesford_Mill" title="Cogglesford Mill">Cogglesford Mill</a> was the only working corn mill in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup> An old mill at the junction of Westgate and Castle Causeway supplied hemp to the growing rope-making business of the Foster and Hill families.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley-50_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley-50-51">[46]</a></sup> As local historian Simon Pawley wrote, "in many respects, things had changed little [by 1783] since the survey of 1692", with few of the buildings or infrastructure being improved.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> Major changes to agriculture and industry took place in the last decade of that century. From the Middle Ages, Sleaford was surrounded by three <a href="/wiki/Open_field_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Open field system">open fields</a>: North, West and Sleaford Fields. At the <a href="/wiki/Enclosure_(legal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclosure (legal)">enclosure</a> of the open fields in 1794 more than 90% of the 1,096&#160;acres of open land was owned by Lord Bristol. Despite the costs of fencing and re-organising the fields, the system was easier to farm, and cottages were built closer to fields, while the landowner could charge more rent owing to the increased profitability of the land; those who lost out were the cottagers, who could no longer keep a few animals grazing on the common land at no cost.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> The process allowed the land boundaries and pathways to be tidied up; Drove Lane, which ran to Rauceby, was shifted north and straightened.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-56">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial">Industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg/220px-Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg.png" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg/330px-Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg/440px-Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="746" data-file-height="569" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sleaford-Map-1891-Simplified.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sleaford, as it appeared in 1891. The major roads are marked in red; railways in grey and rivers in blue. Key: (1) Market Place, (2) St Denys' Church, (3) Manor House, (4) Carre's Grammar School, (5) Westholme House, (6) Castle, (7) Station, (8) Old Place, (9) the remains of St Giles's Church, (10) the Union workhouse.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Canalisation of the River Slea began in the 1790s. <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">Canals</a> in England were constructed from the 1760s to make inland trade easier; Sleaford's businessmen were keen to benefit from the improved communication they allowed. <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Navigation" title="Sleaford Navigation">Sleaford Navigation</a> opened in 1794.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-56">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:19_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-58">[53]</a></sup> It facilitated the export of agricultural produce to the Midlands, and the import of coal and oil. Mills along the Slea benefited and wharves were constructed around Carre Street.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-59">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup> Between 1829 and 1836 the navigation's toll rights increased in value by 27 times.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-59">[54]</a></sup> The railways emerged in the 19th century as an alternative to canals and arrived at the town in 1857, when a line from <a href="/wiki/Poacher_Line" title="Poacher Line">Grantham to Sleaford</a> opened.<sup id="cite_ref-:20_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-61">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:21_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:21-62">[57]</a></sup> It made agricultural trade easier and improved communication,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[n 6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup> but led to the decline of the Navigation Company. Income from tolls decreased by 80% between 1858 and 1868; it made its first loss in 1873 and was abandoned in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_pp._89_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis_pp._89-65">[59]</a></sup> The town's rural location and transport links meant that the late 19th century saw the rise of two local seed merchants: Hubbard and Phillips, and Charles Sharpe; the former took over the Navigation Wharves, and the latter was trading in the US and Europe by the 1880s.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> The railway, Sleaford's rural location and its artesian wells, were key factors in the development of the 13-acre <a href="/wiki/Bass_Maltings,_Sleaford" title="Bass Maltings, Sleaford">Bass &amp; Co</a> <a href="/wiki/Malt_house" title="Malt house">maltings</a> complex at Mareham Lane (1892–1905).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the first half of the 19th century, Sleaford's population more than doubled from 1,596 in 1801 to 3,539 in 1851.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[62]</a></sup> Coinciding with this is the construction or extension of public buildings, often by the local contractors Charles Kirk and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Parry_(Boston_MP)" title="Thomas Parry (Boston MP)">Thomas Parry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[n 7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> The gasworks opened in 1839 to provide gas lighting in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-Mid-Victorian_Sleaford_p._94_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mid-Victorian_Sleaford_p._94-73">[66]</a></sup> Sleaford's <a href="/wiki/Poor_Law_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor Law Union">Poor Law Union</a> was formed in 1836 to cater for the town and the surrounding 54 parishes. The <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a> was constructed by 1838 and could house 181 inmates.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-74">[67]</a></sup> Despite these advances, the slums around Westgate were over-crowded, lacking sanitation and disease-ridden;<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[n 8]</a></sup> the local administration failed to deal with the matter until 1850, when a report on the town's public health by the General Board of Health heavily criticised the situation and set up a Local Board of Health to undertake public works.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley79-80_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley79-80-75">[68]</a></sup> By the 1880s, Lord Bristol had allowed clean water to be pumped into the town, but engineering problems and a reluctance to sell land to house the turbines had delayed the introduction of sewers.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[69]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-industrial">Post-industrial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-industrial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg/220px-Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg/330px-Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg/440px-Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Officer_Training_School_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1554138.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Officer Training School at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Cranwell" title="RAF Cranwell">RAF Cranwell</a>, near Sleaford.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although largely undamaged in the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World Wars</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[70]</a></sup> Sleaford has close links with the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> due to its proximity to several RAF bases, including <a href="/wiki/RAF_Cranwell" title="RAF Cranwell">RAF Cranwell</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Waddington" title="RAF Waddington">RAF Waddington</a>. Lincolnshire's topography—flat and open countryside—and its location on the east of the country made it ideal for the development of Britain's airfields, constructed in the First World War. Work began on Cranwell in late 1915; it was designated an RAF base in 1918 and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force_College_Cranwell" title="Royal Air Force College Cranwell">RAF College</a> opened in 1920 as the world's first air academy.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Royal_Air_Force_Cranwell_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Royal_Air_Force_Cranwell-79">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-raf.mod.uk_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-raf.mod.uk-80">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cranwell_branch" title="Cranwell branch">Cranwell branch</a> railway linking Sleaford station to the RAF base opened in 1917 and closed in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup> During the Second World War, Lincolnshire was "the most significant location for bomber command" and <a href="/wiki/Rauceby_Hospital" title="Rauceby Hospital">Rauceby Hospital</a>, south-west of Sleaford, was requisitioned by the RAF as a specialist burns unit which plastic surgeon <a href="/wiki/Archibald_McIndoe" title="Archibald McIndoe">Archibald McIndoe</a> regularly visited.<sup id="cite_ref-History_of_Royal_Air_Force_Cranwell_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_of_Royal_Air_Force_Cranwell-79">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the inter-war period, Sleaford's population remained static, but the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> in the 1930s caused unemployment to rise.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> Council-housing developments along Drove Lane proved insufficient to house low-income families after the Westgate slums were cleared in the 1930s so Jubilee Grove opened in that decade to meet the demand.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup> In the post-war period, there were new housing developments at St Giles Avenue, the Hoplands, Russell Crescent, Jubilee Grove and Grantham Road.<sup id="cite_ref-n-kesteven.gov.uk_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-n-kesteven.gov.uk-85">[77]</a></sup> Parts of the town were redeveloped: in 1958, the Bristol Arms Arcade opened, the Corn Exchange was demolished in the 1960s and the Waterside Shopping Precinct opened in 1973, as did Flaxwell House, designed to house a department store, though later becoming the national headquarters for <a href="/wiki/Interflora" title="Interflora">Interflora</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1979, the major landowner, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hervey,_6th_Marquess_of_Bristol" title="Victor Hervey, 6th Marquess of Bristol">Victor Hervey, 6th Marquess of Bristol</a>, heavily in debt, had sold most of his estates in Sleaford and Quarrington and the estate's office closed in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley-122_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley-122-87">[79]</a></sup> Much of the land was sold to property developers and in the following decades saw the construction of residential buildings and a considerable population increase.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup> According to a council report, "the quality of life, low crime rates, relatively low house prices and good-quality education" attracted people to the town.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-89">[81]</a></sup> From 1981 to 2011, Sleaford's population rose from 8,000 to 18,000; the growth rate from 1991 to 2001 was the fastest of any town in the county.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup> Its infrastructure struggled to cope, especially with increased traffic congestion; two bypasses around the town and a one-way system were introduced, a process which Simon Pawley argues accelerated the decline of the High Street.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley-122_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley-122-87">[79]</a></sup> In the early 2000s, the Single Regeneration Budget of £15 million granted to Sleaford improved the town centre and funded development of the Hub (since 2011, <a href="/wiki/The_National_Centre_for_Craft_%26_Design" title="The National Centre for Craft &amp; Design">the National Centre for Craft &amp; Design</a>) in the old Navigation wharves area.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Topography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sleaford is the principal <a href="/wiki/Market_town" title="Market town">market town</a> in the <a href="/wiki/North_Kesteven" title="North Kesteven">North Kesteven</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire" title="Lincolnshire">Lincolnshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-93">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Civil_parishes_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil parishes in England">civil parish</a> includes the hamlet of Holdingham to the north east and the village of <a href="/wiki/Quarrington,_Lincolnshire" title="Quarrington, Lincolnshire">Quarrington</a> to the south east, both of which merge with the town.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup> The County Council's <i>State of the Environment Report</i> (1994) found that roughly three-quarters of Lincolnshire is low-lying, with much of it near sea-level;<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup> Sleaford lies approximately 43 feet (13 m) above sea level close to <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cliff" title="Lincoln Cliff">Lincoln Cliff</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_limestone" title="Lincolnshire limestone">Limestone</a> <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">scarp</a> running north–south through <a href="/wiki/Lindsey,_Lincolnshire" title="Lindsey, Lincolnshire">Lindsey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kesteven" title="Kesteven">Kesteven</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24-96">[88]</a></sup> The bedrock under the western half of the town belongs to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Oolite_Group" title="Great Oolite Group">Great Oolite Group</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">Sandstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">Limestone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argillaceous_minerals" title="Argillaceous minerals">Argillaceous</a> rocks formed 168−165 million years ago; <a href="/wiki/Kellaways_Formation" title="Kellaways Formation">Kellaways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Clay_Formation" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford Clay Formation">Oxford Clay</a> formations, dated to 165–156 million years ago, underlie the eastern half.<sup id="cite_ref-:25_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:25-97">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alluvium" title="Alluvium">Alluvium</a> deposits are found along the Slea's course, and Fen sand and gravel are found to the east and south.<sup id="cite_ref-:24_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:24-96">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:25_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:25-97">[89]</a></sup> The county's agricultural land is generally of "very good" quality; as a result, intensive <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a> and vegetable farming is predominant and pastoral farming declined over the course of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> Sleaford is on the edge of <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">the Fens</a>, a low-lying region of the East of England which, before drainage from the 17th to the 20th centuries, were marshy and liable to flooding. Draining has revealed nutrient-rich soils and enabled 88% of the land to be cultivated, especially for arable farming, and most of it is graded amongst the most productive farmland in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_classification" class="mw-redirect" title="Köppen classification">Köppen classification</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> experience a maritime climate characterised by relatively cool summers and mild winters. Lincolnshire's position on the east of the Isles allows for a sunnier and warmer climate relative to the national average, and it is one of the driest counties in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup> Although it may vary depending on altitude and proximity to the coast, the mean average temperature for the East of England is approximately 9&#160;°C to 10.5&#160;°C; the highest temperature recorded in the region was 37.3&#160;°C at <a href="/wiki/Cavendish,_Suffolk" title="Cavendish, Suffolk">Cavendish</a> on 10 August 2003. On average, the region experiences 30 days of rainfall in winter and 25 in summer, with 15 days of thunder and 6–8 days of hail per year; on 25 August 2001, hail the size of golf balls were reported in Sleaford and other parts of central Lincolnshire. Wind tends to affect the north and west of the country more than the East, and Lincolnshire tends to receive no more than 2 days of gale per year (where gale is a gust of wind at &gt;34&#160;knots, sustained for at least 10 minutes). Despite this, tornadoes form more often in the East of England than elsewhere in the country; Sleaford experienced tornadoes in 2006 and 2012, both of which caused damage to property.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[94]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_and_North_Hykeham_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sleaford and North Hykeham (UK Parliament constituency)">Sleaford and North Hykeham (UK Parliament constituency)</a></div>
<p>Before 1832, Sleaford was in the <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Lincolnshire (UK Parliament constituency)">Lincolnshire parliamentary constituency</a>, which encompassed all of the county except for four boroughs. In the 1818 election, 49 of the 2,000 people living in New and Old Sleaford and Quarrington qualified to vote. In 1832, the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act</a> widened the franchise and divided Lincolnshire. Sleaford was in the <a href="/wiki/South_Lincolnshire_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="South Lincolnshire (UK Parliament constituency)">South Lincolnshire constituency</a> that elected two members to parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[97]</a></sup> Following the 1867 reforms, the South Lincolnshire constituency's borders were redrawn, but Sleaford remained within it.<sup id="cite_ref-:9_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:9-106">[98]</a></sup> The franchise was widened by the reforms so that roughly 15% (202) of males in Sleaford and Quarrington could vote in 1868.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[99]</a></sup> The constituency was abolished in 1885 and the <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sleaford (UK Parliament constituency)">Sleaford constituency</a> was formed. It merged with the <a href="/wiki/Grantham_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Grantham (UK Parliament constituency)">Grantham seat</a> in 1918. In 1997, Sleaford was reorganised into <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_and_North_Hykeham_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sleaford and North Hykeham (UK Parliament constituency)">Sleaford and North Hykeham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament#United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">member</a> returned in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010</a> for Sleaford and North Hykeham was the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Phillips_(politician)" title="Stephen Phillips (politician)">Stephen Phillips</a>, who replaced <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hogg" title="Douglas Hogg">Douglas Hogg</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> Phillips was re-elected in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">2015</a>, and supported Britain leaving the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. He resigned in November 2016 when <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>'s Conservative government announced it would appeal a High Court judgement requiring Parliamentary approval prior to the triggering of Britain's exit from the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="Lincolnshire (European Parliament constituency)">Lincolnshire</a> elected a Member of the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> from 1974 until 1994,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup> and then became part of the <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_and_Humberside_South_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="Lincolnshire and Humberside South (European Parliament constituency)">Lincolnshire and Humberside South</a> constituency until 1999;<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup> since then, it has elected members as part of the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Midlands (European Parliament constituency)">East Midlands constituency</a>; from 1999, there were six members for the East Midlands, but the number was reduced to five in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg/220px-North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg.png" width="220" height="264" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg/330px-North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg/440px-North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1086" data-file-height="1305" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Kesteven_UK_locator_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sleaford is in the North Kesteven District of Lincolnshire (coloured red on this map).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/North_Kesteven" title="North Kesteven">North Kesteven</a></div>
<p>From the early medieval period, New Sleaford was in the Flaxwell <a href="/wiki/Wapentake" class="mw-redirect" title="Wapentake">Wapentake</a> and Old Sleaford in the Ashwardhurn Wapentake.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup> Sleaford <a href="/wiki/Poor_Law_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor Law Union">Poor Law Union</a>, overseen by a Board of Guardians, was established in 1836.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-74">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[n 9]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Local_board_of_health" title="Local board of health">Local Board of Health</a> was formed in 1851. Public health was reorganised by the Public Health Act 1872, which established Urban and Rural <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Sanitary District">Sanitary Districts</a> (USD or RSD); Sleaford USD covered New and Old Sleaford, Holdingham and Quarrington, while the Sleaford RSD included all other parishes in the Poor Law Union.<sup id="cite_ref-whites1872_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whites1872-121">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:16_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-122">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[114]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1894" title="Local Government Act 1894">Local Government Act 1894</a> converted the Board of Health and USD into the Sleaford Urban District Council and, in 1899, the town became the administrative base of Kesteven County Council.<sup id="cite_ref-:16_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:16-122">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[115]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._119_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._119-125">[116]</a></sup> A rural district formed in 1894 was abolished in 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[117]</a></sup> In 1973, Sleaford Urban District merged with the North and East Kesteven districts to form North Kesteven, a district of Lincolnshire;<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[119]</a></sup> by statutory instrument, Sleaford civil parish became the urban district's <a href="/wiki/Successor_parish" title="Successor parish">successor</a>, thus merging Quarrington, New Sleaford, Old Sleaford and Holdingham civil parishes.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sleaford Town Council, the parish-level local government body beneath the district council is composed of 18 councillors from six wards: Castle, Holdingham, Mareham, Navigation, Quarrington and Westholme.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[121]</a></sup> The Chairman of the Town Council is also the Mayor of Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[123]</a></sup> The six wards are also represented on <a href="/wiki/North_Kesteven_District_Council" title="North Kesteven District Council">North Kesteven District Council</a>, although Mareham and Quarrington are merged into a single ward.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[124]</a></sup> Sleaford sends one councillor to <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_County_Council" title="Lincolnshire County Council">Lincolnshire County Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[125]</a></sup> Sleaford Town Council has offices on Carre Street and the District Council offices are in the Lafford Terrace building on Eastgate, which was purchased by the council in 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sleaford Urban District Council was granted a coat of arms on 26 October 1950 and after it was abolished the arms were used by its successor, Sleaford Town Council. The arms are blazoned: <i>Gules on a Chevron Or three Estoiles Sable on a Chief Argent as many Trefoils slipped Vert</i>. The trefoils in the chief are from the arms of the Marquess or Bristol, while the lower portion of the shield is the arms of the Carre family. Its crest is blazoned: <i>On a Wreath of the Colours an Eagle wings extended and head downwards and to the sinister proper holding in the beak an Ear of Wheat stalked and leaved Or</i>, the eagle symbolises Sleaford's links with the Royal Air Force and the ear of wheat represents agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_services">Public services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Public services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Policing is provided by the <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_Police" title="Lincolnshire Police">Lincolnshire Police</a>, and fire-fighting by the <a href="/wiki/Lincolnshire_Fire_and_Rescue_Service" title="Lincolnshire Fire and Rescue Service">Lincolnshire Fire and Rescue Service</a>. The police station is on Boston Road, although older premises on Kesteven Street were erected in 1845 and reconstructed in 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[131]</a></sup> The fire station is on Church Lane although plans to move it to East Road by 2016 were approved in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Ambulance_Service" title="East Midlands Ambulance Service">East Midlands Ambulance Service</a> (EMAS) operates from Kesteven Street.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[134]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Lincolnshire_Hospitals_NHS_Trust" title="United Lincolnshire Hospitals NHS Trust">United Lincolnshire Hospitals NHS Trust</a> provides services at three hospitals, <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim_Hospital" title="Pilgrim Hospital">Pilgrim Hospital</a> in Boston, <a href="/wiki/Grantham_and_District_Hospital" title="Grantham and District Hospital">Grantham and District Hospital</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_County_Hospital" title="Lincoln County Hospital">Lincoln County Hospital</a>, all of which have 24-hour accident and emergency departments as of January 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1879, an Act of Parliament was passed to set up a water company for the town; pumping machinery was installed and works constructed in 1880 to provide a clean water supply to the town. In 1948, the council took over the company and in 1962 its operation was handed to the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kesteven_Water_Board&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kesteven Water Board (page does not exist)">Kesteven Water Board</a>, which was absorbed by the <a href="/wiki/Anglian_Water_Authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglian Water Authority">Anglian Water Authority</a> in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>The County Council promoted a Bill to Parliament to build an electricity generating station which passed in 1900. It was built at the cost of £6,700 in 1901 on Castle Causeway and remained there until nationalisation in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[138]</a></sup> Following nationalisation, electricity was provided by the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Electricity_Board" class="mw-redirect" title="East Midlands Electricity Board">East Midlands Electricity Board</a> until it was privatised in 1990; in 1998, East Midlands Electricity, the privately owned predecessor company, was purchased by <a href="/wiki/PowerGen" class="mw-redirect" title="PowerGen">PowerGen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[139]</a></sup> A "virtually carbon neutral" straw-burning power-station at Sleaford opened in 2013; capable of supplying electricity to 65,000 homes, it is powered by straw bales from farms within a 50-mile radius. Most electricity generated is fed into the <a href="/wiki/National_Grid_(Great_Britain)" title="National Grid (Great Britain)">National Grid</a> and the facility provides free heat to public buildings in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sleaford Gas Light Company was formed in 1838 and the following year, gas lighting was provided and a gasworks was constructed on Eastgate. In 1866, the company was incorporated; in 1895–96, the works were rebuilt and lit the town until the company was nationalised in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[141]</a></sup> Gas ceased to be made here in the 1960s and the original buildings were retained although later extensions were demolished from 1966 to 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sleaford Library houses a local and family history section and microfiche machine. It was refurbished in 2010, but, as of 2014, was listed by the county council as "undersized".<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Employment">Employment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Employment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sleaford served the surrounding agricultural communities and the town maintained a weekly market throughout the 19th century and a livestock market on Northgate from 1874 until 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-:23_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:23-93">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[145]</a></sup> According to a 2010 council report, the public sector was the town's main employer, along with agriculture and manufacturing. Unemployment was lower than the national average as were wages reflecting pay in the food processing and agricultural industries.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-89">[81]</a></sup> At the 2011 Census, the largest group of working-age persons by economic activity are those in full-time employment, who make up 43.8% of this section of the population, while 15% are part-time employees and 7.7% are self-employed; 15% of the working-age population are retired. 4.2% were unemployed, with 40% of those in long-term unemployment and roughly one third were aged 16 to 24. The largest <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">socio-economic grouping</a> is those working in lower-tier managerial or administrative roles (21.9%), followed by semi-routine (17.8%), routine (15%) and intermediate (12.5%) occupations; no other group comprised 10% or more. In terms of industry, the most common, based on those working in the sector, are wholesale and retail trade (including automotive repairs) at 16.9%, health and social care (13.4%), public administration and defence (13.3%) and manufacturing (10.9%), with no other groups representing 10% or more.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-1">[1]</a></sup> An unemployment survey of Lincolnshire in 2014 found that the county experienced a decline in unemployment (based on <a href="/wiki/Jobseekers_Allowance" class="mw-redirect" title="Jobseekers Allowance">Jobseekers Allowance</a> claimants) by 29% over the preceding 12 months, while the county's unemployment rate was marginally below the national average.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regeneration">Regeneration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Regeneration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2011 North Kesteven District Council produced a 25-year strategy to regenerate the town because its rapid growth since the 1990s had outgrown improvements to its infrastructure. It plans future residential developments and outlines ways of improving the town centre. It suggests developing more parking around the centre and reverting parts the one-way system, developing southern Southgate and turning Money's Yard into an attraction to link with the National Centre for Craft and Design.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[147]</a></sup> North Kesteven District Council granted planning permission for a £56&#160;m project to redevelop the derelict <a href="/wiki/Bass_Maltings,_Sleaford" title="Bass Maltings, Sleaford">Bass Maltings</a> site by converting it into residential and retail space and creating about 500 permanent jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[149]</a></sup> The development including a supermarket was delayed when the town council opposed a link road through part of the recreation ground.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[151]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tesco" title="Tesco">Tesco</a>, who had pledged to invest in a £20&#160;million store in the development withdrew in January 2015 following financial set-backs.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg/170px-Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg/255px-Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg/340px-Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="1280" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sleaford_Navigation_and_the_Hub_-_geograph.org.uk_-_601972.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/River_Slea" title="River Slea">River Slea</a> in the town was part of the disused <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Navigation" title="Sleaford Navigation">Sleaford Navigation</a> canal</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/A17_road_(England)" title="A17 road (England)">A17 road</a> from <a href="/wiki/Newark-on-Trent" title="Newark-on-Trent">Newark-on-Trent</a> to <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Lynn" title="King's Lynn">King's Lynn</a> bypasses Sleaford from Holdingham Roundabout to <a href="/wiki/Kirkby_la_Thorpe" title="Kirkby la Thorpe">Kirkby la Thorpe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-162">[153]</a></sup> It ran through the town until the bypass opened in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[155]</a></sup> The Holdingham roundabout connects the A17 to the <a href="/wiki/A15_road_(England)" title="A15 road (England)">A15 road</a> from <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a> to <a href="/wiki/Scawby" title="Scawby">Scawby</a>. It also passed through Sleaford until 1993, when its bypass was completed.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[157]</a></sup> Three roads meet at Sleaford's market place: Northgate (B1518), Southgate and Eastgate (B1517). A <a href="/wiki/One-way_system" class="mw-redirect" title="One-way system">one-way system</a> set up in 1994 creates a circuit around the town centre.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-162">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>The railways arrived in the 19th century. Early proposals to bring a line to Sleaford failed,<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[n 10]</a></sup> but in 1852 plans were made to build the Boston, Sleaford and Midland Counties Railway and its <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Act of Parliament</a> passed in 1853. The line from Grantham opened in 1857; <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> was connected in 1859, <a href="/wiki/Bourne,_Lincolnshire" title="Bourne, Lincolnshire">Bourne</a> in 1871 and <a href="/wiki/Ruskington" title="Ruskington">Ruskington</a> on <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_and_Great_Eastern_Joint_Railway" title="Great Northern and Great Eastern Joint Railway">Great Northern and Great Eastern Joint Railway</a> in 1882.<sup id="cite_ref-:20_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:20-61">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:21_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:21-62">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sleaford_railway_station" title="Sleaford railway station">Sleaford</a> is a stop on the <a href="/wiki/Peterborough_to_Lincoln_Line" class="mw-redirect" title="Peterborough to Lincoln Line">Peterborough to Lincoln Line</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Poacher_Line" title="Poacher Line">Poacher Line</a>, from Grantham to Skegness.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[160]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Grantham_railway_station" title="Grantham railway station">Grantham</a>, roughly 14.8 miles (23.8&#160;km) away by road and two stops on the Poacher Line, is a major stop on the <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_Main_Line" title="East Coast Main Line">East Coast Main Line</a>. Trains from Grantham to London <a href="/wiki/London_King%27s_Cross_railway_station" title="London King's Cross railway station">King's Cross</a> take approximately 1 hour 15 minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[162]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/River_Slea" title="River Slea">River Slea</a> through the town was converted into use as a canal for much of the 19th century. Plans to canalise it were drawn up in 1773,<sup id="cite_ref-:3_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-56">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup> but faced opposition from land-owners who feared it might affect the drainage of fens. Plans were approved in 1791 with the support of <a href="/wiki/Brownlow_Bertie,_5th_Duke_of_Ancaster_and_Kesteven" title="Brownlow Bertie, 5th Duke of Ancaster and Kesteven">Brownlow Bertie, 5th Duke of Ancaster and Kesteven</a> who owned estates and quarries that he hoped would benefit. An Act of Parliament passed in 1792, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Navigation" title="Sleaford Navigation">Sleaford Navigation</a>, which opened two years later.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_56-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-56">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:19_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:19-58">[53]</a></sup> After falling revenues due to competition from the railways, the navigation company closed in 1878. The river, although no longer navigable, passes under Carre Street and Southgate.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_pp._89_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis_pp._89-65">[59]</a></sup> The Nine Foot Drain, also unnavigable, meets the Slea just before Southgate.<sup id="cite_ref-:14_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:14-162">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
