<p>The resident population at the 2011 Census was 17,671, which accounts for roughly 15% of the population of the North Kesteven District; the urban area contained 8,690 houses.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-181">[170]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:11_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-182">[171]</a></sup> The town's population grew by 39% between 1991 and 2001, the fastest growth rate of any town in Lincolnshire.<sup id="cite_ref-:12_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-183">[172]</a></sup> The district population is predicted to rise by 29% between 2008 and 2033, compared with a national average of 18%;<sup id="cite_ref-:12_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-183">[172]</a></sup> in 2013, county councillors approved plans to build 4,500 new homes.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-182">[171]</a></sup> A joint planning strategy report found that "This growth has largely been the result of people moving to the area attracted by the quality of life, low crime rates, relatively low house prices and good-quality education."<sup id="cite_ref-:22_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-89">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2011 Census revealed that approximately 93.6% of the town's resident population were White British; the second largest ethnic group was White Irish at approximately 3.4%, followed by Asian (including Asian British) at 1.09%; no other ethnic group represented 1% or more of the population. 88.5% of residents were born in England and 4.41% in other parts of the United Kingdom; 4.3% were from EU countries, with 2.5% coming from EU member states which joined after 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between December 2013 and November 2014, 1,289 criminal acts were reported, of which 43.9% were classed as anti-social behaviour, making it the largest portion of reported crimes.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[173]</a></sup> In 2010, recorded crime levels were amongst the lowest in the country and, for the year ending June 2014, the crime rate in the North Kesteven district is the lowest in Lincolnshire at 24.38 crimes per thousand residents.<sup id="cite_ref-:22_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-89">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg/220px-St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg/330px-St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg/440px-St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:St.Denys%27_nave_-_geograph.org.uk_-_960450.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Constructed in the <a href="/wiki/Decorated_Gothic" class="mw-redirect" title="Decorated Gothic">Decorated Gothic</a> style, much of the nave of St Denys' Church dates to the 14th century.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-186">[175]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most people in the town identify as <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a>, although the proportion has declined between the last two censuses. At the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 Census</a>, 70.3% of residents identified as Christian, while 21.8% reported no religion, and 6.6% did not state a religion; no other religious group comprised 1% or more of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[176]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 Census</a> recorded that 81.6% of Sleaford residents identified as Christian, nearly ten percentage points higher than the national figure (71.8%); 11.5% of the town's residents had no religion and 6% did not disclose a religion.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <i>Compton Census</i> (1676), New Sleaford had a <a href="/wiki/Conformist" title="Conformist">Conformist</a> population of 576 people, no "<a href="/wiki/Papist" title="Papist">Papists</a>" and 6 <a href="/wiki/Non-conformists" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-conformists">Non-conformists</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[178]</a></sup> In the 19th century, it had a sizeable Non-conformist population and a large Anglican congregation; at the 1851 Census of Religious Worship, an estimated 2,000 people attended Non-conformist places of worship, while an estimated 600–700 people attended <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> services in the parish.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._88_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._88-190">[179]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Wesleyan_Methodist_Church_(Great_Britain)" title="Wesleyan Methodist Church (Great Britain)">Wesleyans</a> met in Westgate in the early 19th century; by 1848, the congregation had set up in Northgate, an area known for its taverns and poor tenements.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[180]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecclesiastical_history">Ecclesiastical history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ecclesiastical history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>New Sleaford had a church and priest by the time of the <i>Domesday Book</i> (1086) and the <a href="/wiki/Vicarage" class="mw-redirect" title="Vicarage">vicarage</a> was founded in 1274. During the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth</a> (1649–60), the vicar was expelled and replaced by <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> ministers, the last of whom was removed following the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">Restoration</a> in 1660 and replaced with an Anglican clergyman.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-186">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[181]</a></sup> In 1616, the vicarage was valued at £8 and in 1872 at £180.<sup id="cite_ref-whites1872_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whites1872-121">[112]</a></sup> As of 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_parish" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiastical parish">ecclesiastical parish</a> of St Denys, Sleaford, encloses the town of Sleaford and hamlet of Holdingham north of the railway line and does not include Quarrington.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[182]</a></sup> It falls within the <a href="/wiki/Deanery_of_Lafford" title="Deanery of Lafford">Lafford Deanery</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archdeacon_of_Lincoln" title="Archdeacon of Lincoln">Lincoln Archdeaconry</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Lincoln" title="Diocese of Lincoln">Diocese of Lincoln</a>. The patron is the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Lincoln" title="Bishop of Lincoln">Bishop of Lincoln</a> and the incumbent vicar is the Rev. Philip Anthony Johnson, who was instituted in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-:28_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:28-194">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:29_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:29-195">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>Old Sleaford was in the possession of <a href="/wiki/Ramsey_Abbey" title="Ramsey Abbey">Ramsey Abbey</a> at the time of Domesday and later <a href="/wiki/Haverholme_Priory" title="Haverholme Priory">Haverholme Priory</a>, and was eventually served by a vicar; the church was dedicated either to St Giles or to All Saints. At the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a> (1536–41), the king took over collection of the tithes, eventually leasing them to Thomas Horseman and then selling them to Robert Carre. In the 17th century, the rectory of Quarrington and the vicarage were combined to form the parish of Quarrington with Old Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-Trollope_Sleaford_pp._184.E2.80.93185_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trollope_Sleaford_pp._184.E2.80.93185-197">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[187]</a></sup> The parish boundaries of New Sleaford and Quarrington with Old Sleaford were last altered in 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[189]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Prebendary" title="Prebendary">prebendary</a> of New Sleaford or Lafford had a seat in the <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln Cathedral</a>; it is not known when it was established, but it was confirmed by the Pope in 1146 and 1163,<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[190]</a></sup> and was in the patronage of the bishop. Sleaford's <a href="/wiki/Tithe" title="Tithe">tithes</a> paid to the prebendary were valued at £11 19s. 7d. (£11.98) in 1616. After the enclosure of Sleaford's fields, a farm at Holdingham Anna was allotted to the prebendary in place of the tithes. The Prebendal Court of Sleaford had jurisdiction over New and Old Sleaford and Holdingham to grant <a href="/wiki/Administration_(probate_law)" title="Administration (probate law)">administration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Probate" title="Probate">probate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[192]</a></sup> The parishes of New and Old Sleaford were in the peculiar jurisdiction of the predendary until 1846, when they became part of Aswardhurn and Lafford Rural Deanery. In 1866 they were placed in Aswardhurn and Lafford No. 2 Rural Deanery, from 1884 in the Lafford No. 2 Rural Deanery, the Lafford South Rural Deanery from 1910, and since 1968, the Lafford Rural Deanery.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[193]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Places_of_worship">Places of worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Places of worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Reformed_Church,_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/United_Reformed_Church%2C_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg/220px-United_Reformed_Church%2C_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/United_Reformed_Church%2C_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg/330px-United_Reformed_Church%2C_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/United_Reformed_Church%2C_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg/440px-United_Reformed_Church%2C_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:United_Reformed_Church,_Southgate_-_geograph.org.uk_-_512574.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
United Reformed Church, Southgate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Anglican <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish church</a> is dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Denis" title="Denis">St.&#160;Denys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[194]</a></sup> The oldest parts date to the late-12th century and the <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">broach-spire</a>, built around 1220, is one of the oldest in England.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-186">[175]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[196]</a></sup> Regular Sunday services are held at the parish church.<sup id="cite_ref-:28_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:28-194">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:29_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:29-195">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>Non-conformist meetings took place on Hen Lane (later Jermyn Street) from about 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._88_190-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._88-190">[179]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Congregationalists" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregationalists">Congregationalists</a> who met there constructed a chapel on Southgate in 1867–68; in 1972, it became Sleaford Reformed Church.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[197]</a></sup> Wesleyans first met in the 1790s at the house of Thomas Fawcett on Westgate.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._88_190-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._88-190">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[n 12]</a></sup> They built a chapel nearby in 1802, which was replaced in 1823; it housed the congregation until 1848 when a larger one was built on North Street. It was demolished and replaced by another on the same site in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[199]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptist</a> chapel was built in Old Sleaford in 1811 to house a congregation of 250, it served the <a href="/wiki/Strict_Baptists" class="mw-redirect" title="Strict Baptists">Strict Baptists</a> until possibly the mid-20th century. The premises have been converted into a house.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[200]</a></sup> A Wesleyan Reform Methodist chapel opened in West Banks in 1864, but since 1896 has been occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Fens were increasingly cultivated after the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, prompting migrant Catholic Irish farm-workers to move to the area. By 1879 a <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> missionary, Father Hermann Sabela, was conducting services in the town. A Catholic school and chapel were built in 1881 on land in Jermyn Street and in 1888, Our Lady of Good Counsel Roman Catholic Church, opened beside it.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[202]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[203]</a></sup> The incumbent priest is Father Michael John Bell, who was appointed in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[204]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[205]</a></sup> Mass is held on Sundays and throughout the week.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[206]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sleaford Muslim Community Association has met in St Deny's Church Hall since the early 2000s. Plans to build a prayer hall on Station Road were approved in November 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[207]</a></sup> Protests were planned by the <a href="/wiki/English_Defence_League" title="English Defence League">English Defence League</a>, but were cancelled.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sleaford Spiritualist Church opened in about 1956 on Westgate.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[211]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary">Primary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Primary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Stgeorges-sleaford.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Stgeorges-sleaford.jpg/220px-Stgeorges-sleaford.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Stgeorges-sleaford.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="225" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Stgeorges-sleaford.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Westholme_House" title="Westholme House">Westholme House</a> (1849) has been part of <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Academy" title="St George's Academy">St George's Academy</a> since <i>c</i>. 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-C._Ellis_p._67_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C._Ellis_p._67-224">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[213]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[214]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sleaford has four <a href="/wiki/Primary_school#United_Kingdom" title="Primary school">primary schools</a>. In 1726 William Alvey bequeathed land to fund teaching children in Sleaford. The school and master's house for Alvey's Endowed School, a <a href="/wiki/National_school_(England_and_Wales)" title="National school (England and Wales)">national school</a>, was built in 1851.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[215]</a></sup> New buildings for the infants' school were constructed in 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-k506_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k506-228">[216]</a></sup> William Alvey Church of England School is housed in the same buildings. It became an academy in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[217]</a></sup> St Botolph's School is a Church of England Primary School, which opened at its current site in 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[218]</a></sup> Church Lane School is housed in buildings constructed in 2002, when the original school house was demolished;<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[219]</a></sup> in 2013, it had <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 201 children on roll.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[220]</a></sup> Our Lady of Good Counsel Roman Catholic School had 155 pupils on roll in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1835, there were eight&#160;day schools and three <a href="/wiki/Sunday_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunday schools">Sunday schools</a> in New Sleaford and two daily schools in Old Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[222]</a></sup> An infant school in the old playhouse on Westgate opened in 1855; Wesleyan schools attached to the chapel on North Street accommodated up to 200 pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[223]</a></sup> In addition to private girls' schools, short-lived private schools for boys were established by Mr Herring and Charles Boyer in 1851, Henry Carruthers, and Edwin Reginald Dibben in 1870 in competition with the grammar school.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[225]</a></sup> Charles Kirk built a school and chapel at Quarrington in 1867. It became St Botolph's Primary School and in 2002 moved to a new site.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[226]</a></sup> In 1879, an art school was established in Duke Street in connection with the <a href="/wiki/Science_and_Art_Department" title="Science and Art Department">Science and Art Department</a>; by 1896, two Wesleyan schools and a Catholic school were also in operation.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[227]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secondary">Secondary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Secondary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Kirk_designed_building,_62_Southgate,_Sleaford,_Lincolnshire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Charles_Kirk_designed_building%2C_62_Southgate%2C_Sleaford%2C_Lincolnshire.jpg/220px-Charles_Kirk_designed_building%2C_62_Southgate%2C_Sleaford%2C_Lincolnshire.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Charles_Kirk_designed_building%2C_62_Southgate%2C_Sleaford%2C_Lincolnshire.jpg/330px-Charles_Kirk_designed_building%2C_62_Southgate%2C_Sleaford%2C_Lincolnshire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Charles_Kirk_designed_building%2C_62_Southgate%2C_Sleaford%2C_Lincolnshire.jpg/440px-Charles_Kirk_designed_building%2C_62_Southgate%2C_Sleaford%2C_Lincolnshire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1917" data-file-height="2375" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charles_Kirk_designed_building,_62_Southgate,_Sleaford,_Lincolnshire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
62 Southgate is the oldest part of <a href="/wiki/Kesteven_and_Sleaford_High_School" title="Kesteven and Sleaford High School">Kesteven and Sleaford High School</a><sup id="cite_ref-C._Ellis_p._67_224-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-C._Ellis_p._67-224">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[228]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The town has three secondary schools with sixth forms: <a href="/wiki/Carre%27s_Grammar_School" title="Carre's Grammar School">Carre's Grammar School</a>, a boys' <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar school</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kesteven_and_Sleaford_High_School" title="Kesteven and Sleaford High School">Kesteven and Sleaford High School</a>, a selective academy girls' grammar school, and <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Academy" title="St George's Academy">St George's Academy</a>, formerly St George's College of Technology, and before that Sleaford (County) Secondary Modern School (mixed non-selective <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Comprehensive School">secondary school</a>). The grammar schools are selective and pupils are required to pass the <a href="/wiki/Eleven_plus_exam" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleven plus exam">Eleven plus exam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[229]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[230]</a></sup> St George's is not selective.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[231]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mixed-sex_education" title="Mixed-sex education">co-educational</a> <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Joint_Sixth_Form" title="Sleaford Joint Sixth Form">Joint Sixth Form</a> consortium between Carre's and St George's allows pupils to choose subjects taught at either school.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[232]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carre's Grammar School was founded in 1604 by a bequest of Robert Carre of <a href="/wiki/Aswarby" title="Aswarby">Aswarby</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[233]</a></sup> It has received Specialist Sports and Science statuses,<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[234]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Inspection_Report_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inspection_Report-247">[235]</a></sup> became an <a href="/wiki/Academy_(English_school)" title="Academy (English school)">academy</a> in 2011 and was judged to be "good" by Ofsted in 2013, at which time it had 817 pupils, including the co-educational <a href="/wiki/Sixth_form" title="Sixth form">sixth form</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Inspection_Report_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Inspection_Report-247">[235]</a></sup> Sleaford and Kesteven High School for Girls was established in 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._119_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._119-125">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kelly_1919.2C_p._505_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kelly_1919.2C_p._505-248">[236]</a></sup> It has specialist art status,<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[237]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[238]</a></sup> became an academy in 2011 and was judged to be "good" by Ofsted in 2013, at which time there were 825 pupils on roll, including those in the co-educational sixth form.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[239]</a></sup> St George's opened in 1908 as Sleaford Council School.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[240]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-k506_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k506-228">[216]</a></sup> It has received specialist technology college status, converted to an academy in 2010 and operates a satellite school at <a href="/wiki/Ruskington" title="Ruskington">Ruskington</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ofsted_Report_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ofsted_Report-253">[241]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[242]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[243]</a></sup> St George's had 2,247 pupils on roll in 2012, across both sites and including the sixth form; when assessed by Ofsted in that year, was judged to be "good".<sup id="cite_ref-Ofsted_Report_253-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ofsted_Report-253">[241]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Hub,_Sleaford.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/The_Hub%2C_Sleaford.JPG/220px-The_Hub%2C_Sleaford.JPG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/The_Hub%2C_Sleaford.JPG/330px-The_Hub%2C_Sleaford.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/The_Hub%2C_Sleaford.JPG/440px-The_Hub%2C_Sleaford.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Hub,_Sleaford.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The National Centre for Craft &amp; Design</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/The_National_Centre_for_Craft_%26_Design" title="The National Centre for Craft &amp; Design">The National Centre for Craft &amp; Design</a> opened as <i>The Hub</i> in 2003 with support from a Single Regeneration Budget grant. It attracts 90,000 visitors on average each year<sup id="cite_ref-:27_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:27-256">[244]</a></sup> and houses exhibitions of applied and contemporary art.<sup id="cite_ref-:27_256-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:27-256">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[245]</a></sup> The Playhouse theatre on Westgate was constructed in 1825, and sold in 1856 to be converted into an infants school and later a library and offices. In 1994, Sleaford Little Theatre bought and restored it and in 2000 it opened to the public.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[246]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[247]</a></sup> The Sleaford Picturedrome opened in 1920; the cinema closed in 2000 and the building became a snooker hall and then a nightclub<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[249]</a></sup> that closed in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sleaford Museum Trust was formed in the 1970s to collect and preserve historical artefacts from the town's history. A <a href="/wiki/Heritage_Lottery_Fund" title="Heritage Lottery Fund">Heritage Lottery Fund</a> grant of more than £94,000 in December 2013 allowed the trust to establish a museum on Southgate, which opened in April 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[252]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[253]</a></sup> Sleaford and District Civic Trust was founded in 1972 to "preserve the best features" of the town.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[254]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[255]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a volunteer <a href="/wiki/Twinning_association" class="mw-redirect" title="Twinning association">twinning association</a>, the Sleaford and District Town Twinning Association, which was founded in 1999. The association has created and maintains links and annual visits with <a href="/wiki/Marquette-lez-Lille" title="Marquette-lez-Lille">Marquette-lez-Lille</a> in France since 1999, and with <a href="/wiki/Fredersdorf-Vogelsdorf" title="Fredersdorf-Vogelsdorf">Fredersdorf-Vogelsdorf</a> in Germany since 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[256]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Town_F.C." title="Sleaford Town F.C.">Sleaford Town F.C.</a> played in the <a href="/wiki/United_Counties_League" title="United Counties League">United Counties League Premier Division</a> for the 2014–15 season.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[257]</a></sup> Formed as Sleaford Amateurs F.C. in 1920, the club was renamed Sleaford Town in 1968. In 2007 it moved to its present grounds at Eslaforde Park.<sup id="cite_ref-sleafordfchist_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sleafordfchist-270">[258]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[259]</a></sup> Sleaford Rugby FC's clubhouse opened in 1999 off the A153.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[260]</a></sup> Sleaford Golf Club was founded in 1905 and had roughly 100 members the following year, which increased to 193 in 1911. The original golf course has been altered. In 2014, the club had roughly 600 members.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[261]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[262]</a></sup> Sleaford Cricket Club has grounds at <a href="/wiki/London_Road,_Sleaford" title="London Road, Sleaford">London Road</a>; the earliest record of the club is in 1803.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[264]</a></sup> The town is also home to Bristol Bowls Club,<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[265]</a></sup> an all-discipline gymnastics club founded in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[266]</a></sup> An outdoor lido opened in 1872 on riverside land owned by the Bristol estate but handed over to the community as public baths.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[267]</a></sup> Indoor facilities were built in the 20th century and the old lido became Sleaford Leisure Centre. In 2011 Kesteven District Council received a grant of £2.85 million, to fund reconstruction of the centre and its gym.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[268]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[269]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_media">Local media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Local media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main radio stations for the county are <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_Lincolnshire" title="BBC Radio Lincolnshire">BBC Radio Lincolnshire</a>, broadcasting on 94.9 FM and 104.7 FM frequencies,<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[270]</a></sup> and the commercial station <a href="/wiki/Lincs_FM" title="Lincs FM">Lincs FM</a>, on 102.2, 96.7 and 97.6 FM.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[271]</a></sup> The town's local newspapers are the <i>Sleaford Standard</i> (founded in 1924),<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[272]</a></sup> the <i>Sleaford Advertiser</i> (founded in 1980)<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[273]</a></sup> and the <i>Sleaford Target</i> (founded in 1984).<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[274]</a></sup> Historically, the <i>Sleaford Gazette</i> operated between 1854 and 1960; the <i>Sleaford Journal</i> ran from at least 1884 until it was incorporated into the <i>Gazette</i> in December 1929,<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[275]</a></sup> while the <i>Sleaford Telegraph</i> ran from 1888 to 1889 and the <i>Sleaford Guardian</i> was in print for a year from 1945 to 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[276]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks">Landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Listed_buildings_in_Sleaford" title="Listed buildings in Sleaford">Listed buildings in Sleaford</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg/220px-Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg/330px-Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg/440px-Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="426" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Manor_House_courtyard_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1150761.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Manor_House,_Sleaford" title="Manor House, Sleaford">Manor House</a>, Northgate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A small number of medieval buildings remain standing in the town. <a href="/wiki/St_Denys%27_Church,_Sleaford" title="St Denys' Church, Sleaford">St Denys' Church</a> and St Botolph's in Quarrington date to the 12th and 13th centuries respectively,<sup id="cite_ref-st-denys_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-st-denys-289">[277]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[278]</a></sup> while Sleaford's half-timbered vicarage is 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[279]</a></sup> St Denys' Church is noted for its <a href="/wiki/Tracery" title="Tracery">tracery</a> and its stone <a href="/wiki/Broach_spire" title="Broach spire">broach spire</a> is one of the oldest in England.<sup id="cite_ref-st-denys_289-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-st-denys-289">[277]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cogglesford_Mill" title="Cogglesford Mill">Cogglesford Mill</a> is the only remaining <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermill</a> in town and is a testament to the economic importance of the <a href="/wiki/River_Slea" title="River Slea">River Slea</a> from the late-Saxon period onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[280]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[281]</a></sup> The Bishops of Lincoln used the medieval town as a base, constructing the now-ruined <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Castle" title="Sleaford Castle">Sleaford Castle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._24_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley_Book_of_Sleaford_p._24-34">[30]</a></sup> and as a means of extracting produce and wealth through demesne farming and by granting a market and limited freedoms to the town.<sup id="cite_ref-:17_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:17-35">[31]</a></sup> As a result, the oldest areas are the market place and the four roads which meet there: Northgate, Southgate, Eastgate and Westgate; many 18th and 19th century buildings are found in this area.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[282]</a></sup></p>
<p>Buildings dating from these centuries include William Alvey's <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">baroque</a> house on Northgate,<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[283]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Manor_House,_Sleaford" title="Manor House, Sleaford">Manor House</a> on Northgate inset with medieval masonry,<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[284]</a></sup> and Sessions House on the Market Place.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[285]</a></sup> The Carre family founded the grammar school which was rebuilt in 1834,<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[286]</a></sup> the hospital, rebuilt in 1830,<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[287]</a></sup> and the almshouses, rebuilt 1857,<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[288]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Victorian</a> builders <a href="/wiki/Kirk_and_Parry" title="Kirk and Parry">Charles Kirk and Thomas Parry</a> constructed or added to numerous public buildings and private residences, including Lafford Terrace and their own houses on Southgate and at Westholme.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[289]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg/220px-Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg/330px-Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg/440px-Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="427" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bass_Maltings_-_geograph.org.uk_-_200267.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The derelict <a href="/wiki/Bass_Maltings,_Sleaford" title="Bass Maltings, Sleaford">Bass Maltings</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the Slea was <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canalised</a> in 1794 and the <a href="/wiki/Sleaford_Navigation" title="Sleaford Navigation">Sleaford Navigation Company</a> constructed offices and wharves along Carre Street.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[290]</a></sup> The canal brought trade, while the Gothic-fronted gasworks on Eastgate lit the town from 1839.<sup id="cite_ref-Mid-Victorian_Sleaford_p._94_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mid-Victorian_Sleaford_p._94-73">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Handley_family" title="Handley family">Benjamin Handley</a> and Anthony Peacock financed and benefited from the navigation and founded the bank that took over Alvey's House on Northgate and later added a Baroque extension;<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[291]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[292]</a></sup> Henry Handley, a Member of Parliament, is commemorated by the Handley Memorial on Southgate, a Gothic monument in the style of an <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Eleanor Cross">Eleanor Cross</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[293]</a></sup> During the 1850s, the railways arrived and the station was built in a Gothic style.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">[294]</a></sup> Sleaford's agricultural location and its new transport links encouraged seed trading and malting in the late 19th century: the seed merchant Charles Sharpe's house, The Pines, is on Boston Road.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[295]</a></sup> The massive Bass and Company <a href="/wiki/Maltings" class="mw-redirect" title="Maltings">maltings</a> complex, constructed in brick off Mareham Lane between 1892 and 1905, is grade II* listed and has a frontage more than 1,000 feet long.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[296]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[297]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleafordians">Sleafordians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Sleafordians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Handley_family" title="Handley family">Handley family</a> were well-connected with business; Benjamin Handley was a lawyer, prominent in the Navigation Company and partner in the local bank Peacock, Handley and Kirton.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[298]</a></sup> His son, Henry was M.P. for South Lincolnshire; after his death, the residents erected a monument to him on Southgate.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[299]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Armstrong_Yerburgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Armstrong Yerburgh">Robert Armstrong Yerburgh</a> the son of Rev. Richard Yerburgh, vicar of New Sleaford was twice M.P. for <a href="/wiki/City_of_Chester_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="City of Chester (UK Parliament constituency)">Chester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[300]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Meres" title="Thomas Meres">Sir Thomas Meres</a>, politician was educated at the grammar school.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[301]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sir_Robert_Pattinson" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Robert Pattinson">Sir Robert Pattinson</a>, also educated at the grammar, was M.P. for Grantham and Sleaford and chairman of <a href="/wiki/Kesteven_County_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Kesteven County Council">Kesteven County Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[302]</a></sup></p>
<p>The religious controversialist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Pickworth" title="Henry Pickworth">Henry Pickworth</a> was born in New Sleaford and challenged the opponent of Quakerism <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bugg" title="Francis Bugg">Francis Bugg</a> to an open debate at Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[303]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Austin_(1613%E2%80%9369)" title="John Austin (1613–69)">John Austin</a>, a religious writer, was educated at the grammar school.<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">[304]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Scoffin" title="William Scoffin">William Scoffin</a> served as the town's Presbyterian minister and preached there for more than forty years,<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">[305]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Fawcett_(minister)" title="Benjamin Fawcett (minister)">Benjamin Fawcett</a>, Presbyterian minister, was born and educated at Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[306]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Kippis" title="Andrew Kippis">Andrew Kippis</a>, the Presbyterian minister, biographer and Fellow of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, attended the Grammar School.<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_p._42_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis_p._42-319">[307]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Banister" title="Richard Banister">Richard Banister</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Optician" title="Optician">oculist</a>, practised for 14 years in Sleaford where he trained in <a href="/wiki/Couching_(ophthalmology)" title="Couching (ophthalmology)">couching</a> <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[308]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Andrews_(mathematician)" title="Henry Andrews (mathematician)">Henry Andrews</a> astronomer and astrologer, worked in Sleaford during his youth.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[309]</a></sup></p>
<p>The royalist poet Thomas Shipman was educated at Carre's Grammar School, as was novelist Henry Jackson;<sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_p._42_319-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis_p._42-319">[307]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[310]</a></sup> Joseph Smedley, the actor and comedian, built the theatre in 1824, before settling in the town in 1842, establishing a printing business and dying in North Street;<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[311]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Haslewood_Shannon" title="Charles Haslewood Shannon">Charles Haslewood Shannon</a>, the artist, was born in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[312]</a></sup> The actress and comedian <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Saunders" title="Jennifer Saunders">Jennifer Saunders</a> was born in Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[313]</a></sup> In popular culture, the singer Lois Wilkinson of <a href="/wiki/The_Caravelles" title="The Caravelles">the Caravelles</a> was born in the town;<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[314]</a></sup> glamour model <a href="/wiki/Abi_Titmuss" title="Abi Titmuss">Abi Titmuss</a> grew up in <a href="/wiki/Ruskington" title="Ruskington">Ruskington</a> and was educated at Kesteven and Sleaford High School;<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[315]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bernie_Taupin" title="Bernie Taupin">Bernie Taupin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elton_John" title="Elton John">Elton John</a>'s songwriter, was born in the town.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[316]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Thompson" title="Eric Thompson">Eric Thompson</a> who narrated <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magic_Roundabout" title="The Magic Roundabout">The Magic Roundabout</a></i> television series, was born in a house on Jermyn Street.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[317]</a></sup> In sport, the professional footballer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Wallington_(footballer)" title="Mark Wallington (footballer)">Mark Wallington</a> who played for <a href="/wiki/Leicester_City_F.C." title="Leicester City F.C.">Leicester City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F.C." title="Derby County F.C.">Derby County</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_City_F.C." title="Lincoln City F.C.">Lincoln City</a>, grew up in Sleaford and, after retiring taught Physical Education at St George's Academy.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[318]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sleaford&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Notes</dt>
</dl>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Much of the gold and bronze found in the cemetery was deposited in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> after it was uncovered in the 1880s by excavator George Thomas.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Bishop succeeded a Saxon <a href="/wiki/Thegn" title="Thegn">thegn</a>, Bardi, and held 11 carucates with 29 villeins, 11 borders, 6 sokeman, a church and priest, as well as 8 mills, 1 acre of woodland, 320 acres of meadow and 330 acres of marsh. Ramsey Abbey had been granted land in Sleaford and surrounding villages in <i>c</i>. 1051 and, by <i>Domesday</i>, its fee called <i>Eslaforde</i> consisted of 1 <a href="/wiki/Carucate" title="Carucate">carucate</a>, 1 <a href="/wiki/Soke_(legal)" title="Soke (legal)">sokeman</a>, 2 <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">villeins</a> and 27 acres of meadow; it was sokeland of the abbot of Ramsey's manor of <a href="/wiki/Quarrington,_Lincolnshire" title="Quarrington, Lincolnshire">Quarrington</a>, where he is recorded holding two churches.<sup id="cite_ref-mahany-roffe-11_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mahany-roffe-11-17">[16]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This hypothesis was based on the topography, the granting of a fair, market and burgage tenure in the 12th century, and the "Old" and "New" epithets <sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The earliest references to Old and New Sleaford occur in 13th century documents, which limits their use as evidence for town plantation; the grants of a market and fair in the 12th century do not necessarily indicate a new settlement, but merely the codification and rationalisation of pre-existing economic arrangements; and the diversion of roads like Mareham Lane and the compass-aligned streets provide no chronology even if they indicate a westward migration from Old Sleaford.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was previously sold by the Bishops of Lincoln to <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset</a> and reverted to the crown on his attainder in 1549; <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mary_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Queen Mary I of England">Queen Mary I</a> later sold it to <a href="/wiki/Edward_Clinton,_1st_Earl_of_Lincoln" title="Edward Clinton, 1st Earl of Lincoln">Edward Clinton, 1st Earl of Lincoln</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[43]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Bricks could also be transported more easily, which contributed to the construction of new buildings on West Banks, Grantham Road and London Road (Ellis 1981, p. 87). For a thorough account of the development of West Banks and adjoining roads (Castle Street, Albert Terrace, Martin's Court, Slea Cottages and Watergate) see Ellis 1981, pp. 51–65. Station Road and Nag's Head Passage were also developed in this period (Ellis 1981, pp. 68–69).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The principle buildings being: Sessions House (1831), the grammar school (1834), Carre's Hospital (1830–1846), the gasworks (1839), Navigation House (1838–39), much of Eastgate (including the Alvey School in 1850, and Kingston and Lafford Terraces in 1856 and 1857), the cemetery (1856) and the corn exchange (1857)</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Playhouse Yard, Charles Street, Leicester Street and Cabbage Row being four main examples.<sup id="cite_ref-Pawley79-80_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pawley79-80-75">[68]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sleaford Poor Law Union consisted of the following parishes: Anwick, Asgarby, Ashby de la Launde, Aswarby, Aunsby, Byard's Leap (1861–1930), Billinghay, Blankney, Bloxholm, Brauncewell, Burton Pedwardine, Cranwell, Culverthorpe, Dembleby, Digby, Dogdyke (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1894–1930), Dorrington, Evedon, Ewerby, Great Hale, Little Hale, Haverholme Priory (1861–1930), Heckington, Helpringham, Holdingham, Howell, Kelby, Kirkby Green, Kirkby la Thrope, North Kyme, South Kyme, Leadenham, Leasingham, Martin, Newton, Osbournby, Quarrington, North Rauceby, South Rauceby, Rowston, Roxholm, Ruskington, Scredington, Scopwick, New Sleaford, Old Sleaford, Spanby, Swarby, Swaton, Temple Bruer with Temple High Grange (1861–1930), Thorpe Tillney, Threckingham, Timberland, Walcot (near Billinghay), Walcot (near Folkingham), Welbourn, Wellingore, Scott Willoughby, Silk Willoughby, Wilsford.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-169"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-169">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Proposals to link Sleaford to Ancaster for transporting stone in 1827 did not materialise; works by the Ambergate Company in the 1840s should have extended to Sleaford, but stopped at Grantham in 1850, while opposition from the Navigation Company to another proposal further delayed railway links to the town.<sup id="cite_ref-shaw-79-81_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shaw-79-81-168">[159]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-176">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is the sum total of the Census wards covering Sleaford; they vary at each Census (see citations for each year).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-209">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">After his death, Cornelius Greenwood wrote and published a biography of him, entitled <i>A short account of the late Mr. Thomas Fawcett&#160;: to which is added, the rise and progress of Methodism in Sleaford</i> (1839). The work was listed in <cite class="citation journal">Stampe, George (1896). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mocavo.com/Proceedings-of-the-Wesley-Historical-Society-Volume-1/415919/29#29">"List of Local Histories"</a>. <i>Proceedings of the Wesley Historical Society</i>. <b>1</b> (1): 12.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASleaford&amp;rft.atitle=List+of+Local+Histories&amp;rft.aufirst=George&amp;rft.aulast=Stampe&amp;rft.date=1896&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.jtitle=Proceedings+of+the+Wesley+Historical+Society&amp;rft.pages=12&amp;rft.volume=1&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.mocavo.com%2FProceedings-of-the-Wesley-Historical-Society-Volume-1%2F415919%2F29%2329&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> and has an OCLC number of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldcat.org/title/short-account-of-the-late-mr-thomas-fawcett-to-which-is-added-the-rise-and-progress-of-methodism-in-sleaford/oclc/28682597">28682597</a>.</span></li>
</ol>

<li id="cite_note-:7-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-:7_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-:7_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a (1987), <i>The Compton Census of 1676: A Critical Edition</i>, Oxford University Press, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0197260411" title="Special:BookSources/0197260411">0197260411</a></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASleaford&amp;rft.au=Clapinson%2C+M.&amp;rft.aufirst=A.&amp;rft.aulast=Whiteman&amp;rft.btitle=The+Compton+Census+of+1676%3A+A+Critical+Edition&amp;rft.date=1987&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0197260411&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
